            <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/faqs/">
             FAQs
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>
         <li class="menu-item menu-item-type-post_type menu-item-object-page menu-item-has-children menu-item-17605" id="menu-item-17605">
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/dont-miss-it/">
           dont miss it
          </a>
          <ul class="sub-menu">
           <li class="menu-item menu-item-type-post_type menu-item-object-page menu-item-17965" id="menu-item-17965">

      <div id="content" role="main">
       <div class="post-5017 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail hentry category-greatest-hits-sneak-peek category-sneak-peek tag-alcove tag-bed tag-building tag-built-in-look tag-bunk-bed tag-child-bed tag-childrens-room tag-diy tag-handmade tag-loft tag-rope-ladder tag-shared-boys-room tag-tutorial" id="post-5017">
        <h1 class="entry-title" style="font-size:1.6em;color:#f26649;">
         how to build a loft
        </h1>
        <div class="entry-meta">
         <span class="meta-prep meta-prep-author">
         </span>
         <span class="entry-date">
          Tue 08.23.11
         </span>
         <span class="meta-sep">
         </span>
         <span class="author vcard" style="letter-spacing: 2px; font-size: 10px; color: #00929f;">
          by
          <a class="url fn n" href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/author/ashley/" style="color: #00929f;font-size: 10px; text-decoration:none;" title="View all posts by ashley @ the handmade home">
           ashley @ the handmade home
          </a>
         </span>
        </div>
        <div class="entry-meta">
         <div style="float:left;font-size:10px;width:310px;">
          
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/a-self-imposed-mental-health-day-some-fun-news/" rel="prev">
           a self imposed mental health day + some fun news.
          </a>
         </div>
         <div style="float:right; font-size:10px;width:305px;padding-right:5px;text-align:right;">
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/kays-house-the-kitchen/" rel="next">
           kays house : the kitchen
          </a>
          
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="entry-content">
         <input class="jpibfi" type="hidden"/>
         <p>
          Hello all! Our post today, on how to build
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/the-boys-room-a-loft-alcove/" target="_blank">
           a fun filled loft
          </a>
          for your little ones, is brought to you by my favoritest person. Jamin and I sat down together once we dreamed up this concept, and he came up with the design behind it all. So, Im letting him take this one by the horns today. Feel special. I only had to twist his arm a little.
         </p>
         <p>
          So, without further ado
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/big_boys_loft_room1.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5019" height="900" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/big_boys_loft_room1.jpg" title="big_boys_loft_room" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          Think 1 box on 4 posts. Its that simple.
         </p>
         <p>
          Jamin here today, and let me dispel any false notions up front: Im not sure my dad ever passed on to me how to hammer a nail. Ive learned it all on the fly. So trust me  you, too, can easily build a loft for your kids to love. And two, remember, your kids are only small once, so wether jumping head first into building a loft or going all out with some kids decor, its totally worth it.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/big_boys_loft_room_21.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5021" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/big_boys_loft_room_21.jpg" title="big_boys_loft_room_2" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          With that out of the way lets just jump in two feet first with a list of supplies that youll need. However, keep in mind that this loft is 131 inches in length, by 39 inches in width, by 49 inches high . You can make the loft any size you want, just make sure to adjust your supply and cut lists.
         </p>
         <p>
          <em>
           Helpful hint: when buying your wood, remember that you need to buy
           <strong>
            untreated wood,
           </strong>
           because pressure treated wood does not paint or stain well. You shouldnt have any problem finding untreated wood at the local lumber yard or home depot. The only pieces that will be hard to find in some places are the untreated 44s. You might be unlucky like we were, and have to buy a more expensive type of wood, to find any untreated, as options were limited in our area.
          </em>
         </p>
         <p>
          <strong>
           Here are the supplies we used for our design.
          </strong>
          (All wood is untreated pine, except for the hard-to-find 44s, which had to be Poplar.)
         </p>
         <p>
          2  4 x 4 x 8 cut at 4 feet each (these are the post that hold the loft.)
         </p>
         <p>
          2  2 x 8 x 12 cut at 131 inches (These run the outside length of the box for the loft.)
         </p>
         <p>
          2  2 x 8 x 4 cut at 37 inches (These run the outside width of the box for the loft.)
         </p>
         <p>
          1  2 x 4 x 10 cut to 37 inches (These will be the inside supports for your loft box)
         </p>
         <p>
          2   inch MDF board cut at 65.5 inches
         </p>
         <p>
          2   inch or bigger eye hooks (depends on rope size)
         </p>
         <p>
          3  1 x 2 x 12 cut at 2 feet (giving you a total of 18)
         </p>
         <p>
          1  1 x 2 x 10 cut at 55 inches
         </p>
         <p>
          32   4 inch lag screws
         </p>
         <p>
          1 box of 2  inch wood screws
         </p>
         <p>
          at least 14 feet of rope (choose this based on your weight bearing desires.)
         </p>
         <p>
          <em>
           Another helpful hint: You dont have to cut a single piece of wood, the great workers at your local Home Depot will do it all for you. Just take them the cut sizes.
          </em>
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/beginning_loft.jpg">
           <img alt="" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/beginning_loft.jpg" title="beginning_loft" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          Once you get home with the wood, lay it all out in the room you plan on building the loft in. It is much easier to build it in the room, than the other option of attempting to bring it inside
          <em>
           after
          </em>
          its built.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/DIY_loft.jpg">
           <img alt="" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/DIY_loft.jpg" title="DIY_loft" width="600"/>
          </a>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/beginning_loft.jpg">
           <br/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          1. Lay out all four outside pieces (all pieces that are 2 x 8) and form a rectangle box with them. Make sure the box corners are square then use your lag screws and screw all four pieces together securing the outside of your box. Use two screws for every corner.
         </p>
         <p>
          2. Next take the 2 x 4s you had cut, and space them evenly throughout the inside of the box you have just made. We spaced ours at 26  inches.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/build_a_childrens_sleeping_loft.jpg">
           <img alt="" height="900" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/build_a_childrens_sleeping_loft.jpg" title="build_a_children's_sleeping_loft" width="600"/>
          </a>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/DIY_loft.jpg">
           <br/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          3. Once youve spaced them evenly, screw them in with lag screws at the top edge of the box. Again I would recommend two lag screws on each side. (Total of 4 per board.) Make sure the 2 x 4s are flush with the top edge because they will help support the deck (MDF board) of the loft.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/d_how_to_build_a_loft.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5025" height="900" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/d_how_to_build_a_loft.jpg" title="d_how_to_build_a_loft" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          4. Once your box, with inside supports or 2 x 4s, is screwed together, lay your MDF board on top and screen it in with the 2  inch wood screws. I recommend at least 5 screws on each beam and 2 in between each beam. (However, in my book, theres no such thing as too many screws.)
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3376_2.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5026" height="900" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3376_2.jpg" title="IMG_3376_2" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          Your box should be complete with decking.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3377.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5027" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3377.jpg" title="IMG_3377" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3379.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5028" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3379.jpg" title="IMG_3379" width="600"/>
          </a>
          5. Next, find some friends, or people that owe you favors. In my case I called on the minions. {Affectionately known as our youth ministry kids} Ashley couldnt help me-it was all kinds of heavy. Youll need help in lifting the box and placing it on the 4 x 4 posts.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3382_2.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5029" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3382_2.jpg" title="IMG_3382_2" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          Once you have gathered your work crew (props, Mark) lift the box and place a 4 x 4 in each corner. {This was Ashleys job. To say she was nervous was an understatement. She made me promise not to drop it on her, but I assured her Id upped her life insurance :)}
         </p>
         <p>
          6. Make sure to push, knock, or flat out beat the post flush into those corners. Then take a lag screw and screw in through the top of the MDF down into the 44. We would have a photo of this, but that included us knocking it into position while Ashley screamed at us not to crush ourselves.
         </p>
         <p>
          7. Next, take a lag screw and screw into the side of the 2 x 8 running both ways of the corner. In total, I would have at least 5 lag screws in each 4 x 4:
         </p>
         <p>
          -1 coming from the top
         </p>
         <p>
          -2 coming from the long edge of the loft box (in my case the 131 inch sides)
         </p>
         <p>
          -2 coming from the short edge of the loft box(in my case the 37 inch sides.)
         </p>
         <p>
          *Repeat the above step for all four 44 posts.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3385.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5030" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3385.jpg" title="IMG_3385" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          Before you know it, your platform is done. Youd never know from the finished photo, that this is a simple box on four posts. My mom saw it in person this last weekend, and thought it was built into the walls. The beauty of this plan is that it disguises well with the curtains that Ashley made, for this space to double as an alcove/play area. Also, when we decide to move, if we move it with us, the only thing we have to do is dissassemble it just like any old Ikea bed.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3387.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5031" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3387.jpg" title="IMG_3387" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          <strong>
           The Railing: because we wouldnt want anyone falling
          </strong>
         </p>
         <p>
          You will need all the 12s you had cut at HD.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3389.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5032" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/IMG_3389.jpg" title="IMG_3389" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          1. Take all the 2 foot pieces and nail them to the 2 x 8 loft support, spacing them at 5 inches. Put 2 nails in the bottom of the 2 x 8 and 2 in the top of the 2 x 8. Repeat this 9 times then start the process over from the other end of the loft. The goal: is to have a 22 inch space in between both sets of 9 1 x 2s spaced at 5 inches.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/loft_railing.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5034" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/loft_railing.jpg" title="loft_railing" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          2. Then take your two 1 x 2s cut to 55 inches, and nail from the top down into each 1 x 2 cut at 2 feet. We decided to make the inside edge flush, leaving the lip on the outside of the loft. You can do it either way, its just preference.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/bedding21.jpg">
           <img alt="" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/bedding21.jpg" title="bedding2" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          The tricky part for us, came when we realized our foundation, of all things, was not level. We had to readjust a little, from the bottom, up, when it became apparent as we installed our rails. We leveled it out with the top rail, once they were readjusted from the bottom. You could tell, because they were uneven at the top, even though they started at the same place from the bottom. It all became better camouflaged, after it was painted.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/loft.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5035" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/loft.jpg" title="loft" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          Your loft is done except for the paint and ladder. I would recommend painting the loft before attaching the ladder. Thats just my personal preference.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/ladder1.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5036" height="900" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/ladder1.jpg" title="ladder" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          Now for the fun rope ladder
         </p>
         <p>
          <em>
           First, some legal jargon: I was unable to find any rope at the local hardware store that didnt contain some semblance of the following phrase. do not use this rope to bear human weight. I choose to ignore this phrase. However, on the recommendation of our lawyers, I think this is a poor choice on my part, and do not advocate nor condone anyone else ignoring this phrase. In fact, I think you would be down right silly to not heed this warning and am questioning my sanity in not abiding by the advice given. So in plain terms in no way does the handmadehome.net, its affiliates, writers, creators, sponsors, children, pets, random friends, readers, web hosts or inanimate projects condone the use of aforementioned rope. But it worked for us.
          </em>
         </p>
         <p>
          Now that we have that out of the way, we can get on to the simplicity of building a rope ladder with the approved rope that you found, and I couldnt:
         </p>
         <p>
          1. I had a piece of 1 x 4 x 8 cut into 22 inch pieces. This gave me four.
         </p>
         <p>
          2. I then drilled a  inch hole through each side, about 1 inch from the edge.
         </p>
         <p>
          3. Starting at what would be the bottom rung I fed the rope through and tied a knot, any self-tightening knot will do. (Find a local boy scout to assist you, if need be.) Move your way up each board with more knots.I spaced my boards at about 10 inches.
         </p>
         <p>
          4. Once you have done both sides, not forgetting to make sure the boards are level you will be ready to attach the rope ladder.Take your eye hooks and screw them into the MDF board about  to 1 inch back from the edge. (Drilling a hole first will make this easier.) Make sure that they will screw into not only the MDF, but the 28 as well.
         </p>
         <p>
          Once screwed in, feed the rope through the eye hook. Tie it off, and cut the excess.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/how_to_build_a_loft2.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5037" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/how_to_build_a_loft2.jpg" title="how_to_build_a_loft2" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          We used
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/05/how-to-make-your-own-window-hardware/" target="_blank">
           this method
          </a>
          to create the alcove by simply placing one pipe into a smaller one, and extending it all the way across. This served as a make shift curtain rod. For a how-to on these curtains, take a look
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/a-practical-approach-how-to-sew-diagonal-borders/" target="_blank">
           here.
          </a>
          We minimized drooping in the middle by screwing in a hook in the center, to support the rod.
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/hook_to_support_centerpiece.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5038" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/hook_to_support_centerpiece.jpg" title="hook_to_support_centerpiece" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          And there it is. (Yep. We do need to touch that hook up. We spray painted it with copper spray paint to blend nicely, but some of it came off when we muscled it into the wood.)
         </p>
         <p>
          The total cost for this project was a bit more than we originally anticipated, at about 250. The posts at the bottom alone, accounting for half the cost. (Ouch.) But we sold the old furniture that was in the space to fund this one, and if you purchased a plain old bunk bed, it would be much, much more than this amount. So all in all, we say it was a great, price efficient project.
         </p>
         <p>
          Once all the paint has dried, and the fabric + bedding has been placedstep back and enjoy your new loft.
         </p>
         <p>
          <em>
           **** For the bed, we used a twin sized mattress, and Malones crib mattress, as he was still small enough. We simply put a body pillow on one side to fill in the gap. It still works, but we will be ordering a custom mattress to fill that spot, soon.
          </em>
         </p>
         <p>
          And be sure to
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/the-boys-room-a-loft-alcove/" target="_blank">
           tour the entire room, here!
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/big_boys_loft_room2.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5039" height="900" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/big_boys_loft_room2.jpg" title="big_boys_loft_room" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          As always, let us know if you try this. We want to see your photos! Hope you found this useful, and that you enjoy building something that will create some great memories for your kids!
         </p>
         <p>
          <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/kids_playing_in_loft_bed1.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-5041" height="400" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-content/uploads/2011/08/kids_playing_in_loft_bed1.jpg" title="kids_playing_in_loft_bed" width="600"/>
          </a>
         </p>
         <p>
          Ours certainly love it!
         </p>
         <br/>
        </div>

             <div class="comment-body">
              <p>
               We ran into a few issues with baseboard heaters and a window. I put a bed guardrail in front of the window in the back of the bed( we talked about the railing going all across the back but we will try this first) Its covers most of the window fine. We doubled up the end piece and one side to give us clearance for the baseboard heater before attaching the legs. I made a book ledge at each end for the boys to have a shelf for a book and some treasures. Now if I can get them to sleep in it
              </p>

              </span>
             </div>
             <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
              <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/how-to-build-a-loft/#comment-88379">
               November 24, 2012 at 10:43 pm
              </a>
             </div>
             <div class="comment-body">
              <p>
               Just a heads up, I recommend sticking with untreated wood. Treated wood is soaked in chemicals to make it highly weather resistant. It will leave a residue on your hands and often has warning labels on it saying you should wear gloves when handling. You wouldnt want your kids bed made of this stuff.
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="reply">
             </div>

           <div class="comment-body">
            <p>
             Hi! I am sooo excited/relieved that I came across this today. I have been obsessively scouring Craigslist in search of an Ikea Kura kids loft bed for the past month, with NO luck! This tutorial is a perfect alternative!!
             <br/>
             I have 2 questions, and I hope they werent already answered and I just missed them:
             <br/>
             1) Can you give me an estimated cost for this project? Just for the most basic parts (the wood and main hardware, not the extras, such as the curtain and ladder)
             <br/>
             2) Is this incredibly sturdy? In more specific words: Would YOU sleep underneath it without worrying about it falling and crushing your face? (I apologize for my horrific anxiety-driven imagination.) My vision: I want to put a queen mattress on the floor, poking out from under the loft. My 4 year-old would sleep on the loft, while my 2 year-old and I sleep underneath on the queen mattress. Our heads and chest would be under the loft, while the rest of our bodies would poke out. So back to my question  would you personally feel safe sleeping like this given the construction of this particular loft? Of course I would NEVER consider this LEGAL advice (lol)  just friendly, parent-to-parent advice.
            </p>
            <p>
             PS- I feel like I should explain that I believe in co-sleeping, thats why I want to do this, not because my kids and I are living in squalor

               <div class="comment-body">
                <p>
                 Hey Wendy! We are actually about to build a different ladder, so right on with that one. ;} The rope has proven to be much to wiggly. My 6 year old can navigate it but its just not suitable for their ages, particularly when sleepy. My son was 2.5 when he started sleeping in it with his big brother. Aiden was almost 6. However, we line both sides with pillows to protect them from the railing because they are crazy sleepers, and we close up the opening for the ladder with one big body pillow so hopefully, they wont go through it. That being said, its not completely fall proof, but it helps. Our youngest has gotten used to it now, but I may have waited a little longer if I could have! Im a huge proponet of keeping kids in cribs as long as possible. ;} It does have its benefits, as in, its still like a crib for our youngest. Once hes in it, he doesnt get down until we come to get him. I hope this helpsI think it just all depends on the children!
                </p>

           <div class="comment-body">
            <p>
             Great tutorial . . . thanks so much for taking the time to share it!
            </p>
            <p>
             And, fwiw, you can double up 24s to make a 44. The combined support strength of 2 24s is something like 1600lbs, so just put a few screws through one and into the other, and youre good to go. (And at $1-2 each, using doubled 24s makes the price hard to beat. ;o)
            </p>
            <p>
             Keep up the great work!
            </p>

Either way, when I build a loft, if I paint the posts, Ill make sure I pressure wash the surface of the 4x4s and then apply an oil-based primer before painting. Thats the tip for all of you other DIY-ers. Heres a link to show you how to do it right:

             <div class="comment-author vcard">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-40 photo" height="40" src="//1.gravatar.com/avatar/42a68d5d1feb297ae8f3a9936e4d9863?s=40&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thehandmadehome.net%2Fwordpress%2Fwp-content%2Fthemes%2Fhandmadehome2013%2Fimages%2Fhmh-avatar.jpg&amp;r=g" srcset="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/42a68d5d1feb297ae8f3a9936e4d9863?s=80&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thehandmadehome.net%2Fwordpress%2Fwp-content%2Fthemes%2Fhandmadehome2013%2Fimages%2Fhmh-avatar.jpg&amp;r=g 2x" width="40"/>

               says:

             <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
              <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/how-to-build-a-loft/#comment-88016">
               November 23, 2012 at 9:20 pm
              </a>
             </div>
             <div class="comment-body">
              <p>
               Jammi,
              </p>
              <p>
               While reading your post, as well as others, I came up with a pretty cool idea which may work as a replacement to the rope ladder. If there is enough room on the side near the foot of the mattress, people could assemble an attic -type of ladder which can be raised and lowered. Now, I really havent developed all the mechanics behind it yet, but it could involve springs like an attic door, simple pulley/counterweight system, or just a simple rope that the kids could pull up or let the ladder-hatch down. To secure it in place when its up, you could use two large bolt latches on the top that would be able to support a decent amount of weight. This way, the kiddos could get away from the younger ones. Now, for those that have smaller ones on top, you could also put latches on the bottom side so that they cant release the latches on their side and fall out.
              </p>
              <p>
               Also, by doing this, you could put a guard rail ALL the way across, reducing roll-out potential and negate having to put a pillow or other obstacle to block the rope-ladder top entrance. Let me know what yall think.
              </p>
              <p>
               -S
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="reply">
             </div>
            </div>
           </li>
          </ul>

           <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">

             October 29, 2011 at 5:51 am
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-body">
            <p>
             great idea! Can I suggest a couple things? deck hangers would have worked great for the middle supports under the MDF. they can be found with decking hardware and are a better way to support the weight of the deck material (and kids).
            </p>
            <p>
             As far as the legs. for a cheaper option you could wrap them in fabric or leather vinyl so painting the Pressure treated stuff would not have been a concern. make sure you cut an X in the material of choice where you plan to screw through. a washer will give a finished look to the interior corners. this step should cut the cost of the project significantly.
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="reply">
           </div>

             <div class="comment-body">
              <p>
               On the ones that you cannot come from the outside of the 28s into the 44s you can just come from the inside of the 44s into the inside of the 28s with longer lag screws. Make sure you have a good drill, with good torque to do this though, otherwise youll never make it through.
              </p>

                   <br/>
                   So really they can be any size you want them to be or your mattress calls for them to be. Make sense? Feel free to email me directly,

            <p>
             Regarding the mattress sizes, you could easily have a piece of foam cut to the appropriate size to fit all the way across. Foam can be cut in any width and length, and also you can pick how hard or soft you want it. Then it is wrapped in a fabric covering. For kids, a foam mattress is great.
            </p>

           <li class="comment byuser comment-author-jamin even depth-2" id="li-comment-40972">
            <div id="comment-40972">
             <div class="comment-author vcard">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-40 photo" height="40" src="//2.gravatar.com/avatar/ebca67725b7098ceefecfd1e074d8910?s=40&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thehandmadehome.net%2Fwordpress%2Fwp-content%2Fthemes%2Fhandmadehome2013%2Fimages%2Fhmh-avatar.jpg&amp;r=g" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/ebca67725b7098ceefecfd1e074d8910?s=80&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thehandmadehome.net%2Fwordpress%2Fwp-content%2Fthemes%2Fhandmadehome2013%2Fimages%2Fhmh-avatar.jpg&amp;r=g 2x" width="40"/>
              <cite class="fn">
               jamin
              </cite>
              <span class="says">
               says:
              </span>
             </div>
             <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
              <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/how-to-build-a-loft/#comment-40972">
               April 8, 2012 at 9:15 pm
              </a>
             </div>
             <div class="comment-body">
              <p>
               The easiest way would be to build two boxes, butt them together and use a 44 in every corner using a total of 8 44s. (You can secure the two lofts together with some lag screws.)
              </p>
              <p>
               If you have some basic building skills you could lose a couple of those 44s by building one of the boxes and placing a 44 in every corner. Then build the second box using a 44 in each of the corners not butted up to the 1st box and screw the side without the 44s into the 1st box. Keep in mind you will need a few extra hands to hold the frame/box up and keep it level. (also make sure you use big enough lag screws and enough of them. At least one every 3 inches, alternating top and bottom, because I like over kill.)
              </p>
              <p>
               The first way will provide a lot more stability and with older boys Id probably choose the 1st route.
              </p>
             </div>
             <div class="reply">
             </div>
            </div>

            <p>
             I wanted to say a big THANK YOU! Your loft was exactly what we were looking to do in our 4 year old sons new room. We were moving him because we are expecting a little girl this September and wanted to use the same room as a nursery. However, we didnt want our son to feel displaced, so we really wanted something to make it special. This was just perfect, the only adjustments we had to make is our room width was only 122 inches, but otherwise the cut list, etc were great! Since he wont be sharing the loft, one end has his bed, and the other end has his bookcase and pillows to cuddle and lay on as a reading nook. We decided to forgo the rope ladder (it made me a little nervous if he got up in the middle of the night) and we just did a simple leaning ladder with 2x6s so they are wide steps. Anyway, I really just wanted to thank you for sharing your plans and making them so easy to follow and all the great pictures that really helped during the building process!

His reaction was priceless and he loves his room (his sister is a tad envious as well). The bed only took about 4 hours to build, 2 hours for the ladder (we went with a ladder more like the one on your outside tree house plans) and then 4-6 hours to paint everything.              February 22, 2013 at 9:45 am
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="comment-body">
            <p>
             How strong are your railings?
             <br/>
             I had to make my kids bed taller to clear a radiator we dont use and im afraid they are going to break the railing leaning on it. Once I get it up Ill test it myself but since yours has been us for awhile I wanted to know how it was going.
            </p>
            <p>
             Also for anyone trying to do this alone I was able to screw in small pieces of MDF in the corners to mount the posts and bolt them in and then removed them to put up the MDF. When I attempted it with the MDF on the base it was too heavy to lift.
            </p>
           </div>

           <div class="comment-body">
            <p>
             Hey there! I happened to stumble upon your blog here and I love this idea! In fact, the whole reason I arrived here was because I was searching on how to do this very thing, well at least something similar. My teenage boys share a room and now that they are in high school they need more space for desks and such than the 2 twin beds on the floor provide, so enter the loft bed. A couple of questions though, for starters I am actually building 2 of the sides and lag-bolting them directly into the studs in the wall. This way, I only need 1 post for the free hanging side. I am assuming that you did not do it this way as you did not want to tear up the walls? Are there any reasons I should not do it the way I was thinking? Also, why the 2 x 8s on the length supports? Was there merely for looks or because it was so long? I ask because I had just planned on using 2 x 4s for the box iteself, again with the lag bolts on the 2 walled sides, with perpendicular 2 x 4s for the shelf support, spaced 12 apart and I would attach those with Perlin brackets (think joist brackets but for 2 x 4s and they wrap over the top). Any concern you could see with this? I had read somewhere that a 2 x 4 over a 4 ft span has a vertical crush weight of something like 20 tons! Maybe I am wrong. Anyhow, our length is going to be shorter than what it looks in your project so I am thinking it might be ok. Thanks again for the interesting post!
            </p>
            <p>
             Mike
            </p>

             <div class="comment-body">
              <p>
               Yes you can screw it into the studs, we choose not to for stability reasons. It is stronger sitting on 4 x 4s. Same thing with the 2 x 8. The price difference between a 2 x 4 and 2 x 8 really isnt much, we choose the few extra dollars for safety and look. I hope that helps and always remember our
               <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/disclosure/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">
                disclosure
               </a>
               about projects.
              </p>

               Jill,
              </p>
              <p>
               Yes it should work for two twins, just adjust the wood lengths to fit two twins butted together.
              </p>
              <p>
               As for cross support, test it out and see. You can always add that at the end, but if you arent bracing it to a wall Id guess yes you will need 4 similar to the ones we used on our pergola,
               <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2013/01/how-to-build-a-pergola/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">
                here
               </a>
               .

            </p>
            <p>
             <a href="http://www.homedepot.com/p/Simpson-Strong-Tie-Z-Max-1-1-2-in-x-2-in-x-2-3-4-in-Angle-A23Z/100375310#.UgxBVD9aCCI" rel="nofollow">
              http://www.homedepot.com/p/Simpson-Strong-Tie-Z-Max-1-1-2-in-x-2-in-x-2-3-4-in-Angle-A23Z/100375310#.UgxBVD9aCCI
             </a>
            </p>
            <p>
             Do you think these would be strong enough for the loft bed frame also?
            </p>
            <p>
             Thanks
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="reply">
           </div>
          </div>

             <div class="comment-body">
              <p>
               Heather,
              </p>
              <p>
               The only difference between a hanging bed and loft are the four corner post, so if they are using them in the hanging bed I would think they would work with a loft. However, if you are just doing a loft, I see no real need for them. Send me an email if you need more help.
              </p>
              <p>
               (And as always guys and gals be sure to read our full disclosure on building,
               <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/disclosure/" rel="nofollow">
                here
               </a>
               .)
              </p>
              <p>
               Jamin

           <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
            <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/how-to-build-a-loft/#comment-152815">
             August 17, 2013 at 6:14 pm
            </a>
           </div>
           <!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->
           <div class="comment-body">
            <p>
             Hey folks, dont fret (or even consider yourself unlucky) if you cant find 44 lumber:  you can always stack two 24s together (on the 4 face) to achieve the same.  Glue, drywall screws and putty/wood plugs should work fine for most applications.
            </p>
            <p>
             Granted, depending on
             <i>
              where
             </i>
             you use them, you may need some additional trim, and yes, it will add a little extra time to your project.  However, you may even save some cash!
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="reply">

             <div class="comment-body">
              <p>
               Yes you do need to pre-drill The size and depth depends on the size of the lag screw. The width should be slightly less than the screw and the same depth as the screw.
              </p>
              <p>
               Hope this helps.
              </p>

            <p>
             Thanks so much for these plans.  Used them to make loft for kids and worked great.  Dimensions were quite a bit different, but turned out great!  Id recommend that anyone building secure the beams to the wall studs for added safety and security.  Also, they make lag screws with a spider head (drill bit included) so they lag screws end up flush with the beam.  Not a huge deal, but looks better.
             <br/>
             TWS

         <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-442819">
          <div id="comment-442819">
           <div class="comment-author vcard">
            <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-40 photo" height="40" src="//2.gravatar.com/avatar/b16c229454d22174f2993c1f08a4805b?s=40&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thehandmadehome.net%2Fwordpress%2Fwp-content%2Fthemes%2Fhandmadehome2013%2Fimages%2Fhmh-avatar.jpg&amp;r=g" srcset="http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/b16c229454d22174f2993c1f08a4805b?s=80&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thehandmadehome.net%2Fwordpress%2Fwp-content%2Fthemes%2Fhandmadehome2013%2Fimages%2Fhmh-avatar.jpg&amp;r=g 2x" width="40"/>
            <cite class="fn">
             Alan Moore
            </cite>
            <span class="says">
             says:
            </span>
           </div>
           <!-- .comment-author .vcard -->
           <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
            <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/2011/08/how-to-build-a-loft/#comment-442819">
             June 26, 2014 at 8:01 am
            </a>
           </div>
           <!-- .comment-meta .commentmetadata -->
           <div class="comment-body">
            <p>
             I made one! Thanks for the ideas.
            </p>
            <p>
             I did modify it a bit.. not just the dimensions to fit the room, but I also changed the design slightly, to adapt it to our houseboat:
            </p>
            <p>
              Firstly, I didnt use a board of MDF on top. On a boat we need to be extra careful about damp and airflow, so I used slats  just thinner planks cut to the width of the bed, and screwed down into the main side boards.
            </p>
            <p>
              Secondly, the walls of the bedroom are not as solid as those in a house, so I added an extra side plank on the three wall sides to further stiffen the bed, so it stands by itself.
            </p>
            <p>
              I found that the head and foot end planks were easier to affix, and looked neater from underneath the bed, if you bolted them to the INSIDE of the bed rather than the wall side. You end up with a blank wood surface with just bolt heads (looking rather like ship rivets) showing.
            </p>
            <p>
              And finally I built a solid step ladder. The rope ladder just wasnt cutting it  partly because the boat moves; and partly because ours is a bit taller. It was very difficult to climb up  and a heavy person would actually tilt the bed over.
            </p>
            <p>
             The side rails for the wooden ladder are two long pieces of 24 with the bottom ends cut at a slight angle, so that they rest flat on the floor and lean at a slight angle against the bed. For the rungs I cut a long sturdy 2 dowel, into five equal sections. I used a hole saw to dig 2 holes in the 24 at regular intervals  the crucial part here is to not go all the way through.. you want your dowel embedded about three quarters of the way through the side rail  then after you put the dowel in, screw in from the outside to secure the end of the rung.
            </p>
            <p>
             The whole ladder is then leant against the bed and screwed into place with big 5 screws. We used two on the feet as well, to secure them to the wooden floor. It helps keep the whole bed in place.
            </p>
            <p>
             The structure is incredibly strong and stable, and is now part of the fabric of the boat. It can easily support the whole family  although our 6 year old complains if both Mum and Dad get in with her
             <img alt=":-)" class="wp-smiley" src="//www.thehandmadehome.net/wordpress/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;"/>
            </p>
            <p>
             Thanks again for the great design ideas!
            </p>
            <p>
             Alan
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="reply">
           </div>

              <p>
               I once saw a grown man roll out of a bunk bed, it looked liked it hurt. I think the railing issue is a personal choice. If you are only using two walls I would add a 3 support or make sure you are ending at a stud to drill into for support on the non post end. As always though
               <a href="http://www.thehandmadehome.net/disclosure/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">
                please read our disclosure about building
               </a>

                 Just thought I would throw this out there as a solution for those wondering about the mattress situation. Ikea sells adjustable mattresses that we are planning to use, so we will basically have two of the larger adjustable mattress pieces (crib length with twin width) and add however much of the additional length pieces we need to fill in the space.
                </p>
